<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genomic effects of population collapse in a critically endangered ironwood tree Ostrya rehderiana"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2018 9:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-12-21"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Increased human activity and climate change are driving numerous tree species to endangered status, and in the worst cases extinction. Here we examine the genomic signatures of the critically endangered ironwood tree Ostrya rehderiana and its widespread congener O. chinensis. Both species have similar demographic histories prior to the Last Glacial Maximum (LGM); however, the effective population size of O. rehderiana continued to decrease through the last 10,000 years, whereas O. chinensis recovered to Pre-LGM numbers. O. rehderiana accumulated more deleterious mutations, but purged more severely deleterious recessive variations than in O. chinensis. This purging and the gradually reduced inbreeding depression together may have mitigated extinction and contributed to the possible future survival of the outcrossing O. rehderiana. Our findings provide critical insights into the evolutionary history of population collapse and the potential for future recovery of the endangered trees. Here, Liu et al. compare the genomic signatures of two tree species, one of which had undergone population collapse. The declining species had low genetic diversity, but had more strongly purged severely deleterious recessive variations, likely due to inbreeding and perhaps mitigating extinction risk."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Increased human activity and climate change are driving numerous tree species to endangered status, and in the worst cases extinction. Here we examine the genomic signatures of the critically endangered ironwood tree <i>Ostrya rehderiana</i> and its widespread congener <i>O. chinensis</i>. Both species have similar demographic histories prior to the Last Glacial Maximum (LGM); however, the effective population size of <i>O. rehderiana</i> continued to decrease through the last 10,000 years, whereas <i>O. chinensis</i> recovered to Pre-LGM numbers. <i>O. rehderiana</i> accumulated more deleterious mutations, but purged more severely deleterious recessive variations than in <i>O. chinensis</i>. This purging and the gradually reduced inbreeding depression together may have mitigated extinction and contributed to the possible future survival of the outcrossing <i>O. rehderiana</i>. Our findings provide critical insights into the evolutionary history of population collapse and the potential for future recovery of the endangered trees.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The combined effects of changing climate and human activities are assumed to have reduced species throughout the world to critically small population sizes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Miraldo, A. et al. An Anthropocene map of genetic diversity. Science 353, 1532â1535 (2016)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39496e595">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Royal Botanic Gardens (Kew). The State of the Worldâs Plants. (Royal Botanic Gardens, Kew, UK, 2016)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39496e595_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cronk, Q. Plant extinctions take time. Science 353, 446â447 (2016)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39496e595_2">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Xu, S. H. et al. The origin, diversification and adaptation of a major mangrove clade (Rhizophoreae) revealed by whole-genome sequencing. Natl. Sci. Rev. 4, 721â734 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39496e598">4</a></sup>. For example, during the last glacial maximum (LGM), the ranges of most temperate species moved south and contracted as the temperature decreased<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Davis, M. B. &amp; Shaw, R. G. Range shifts and adaptive responses to Quaternary climate change. Science 292, 673â679 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d39496e602">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Provan, J. &amp; Bennett, K. Phylogeographic insights into cryptic glacial refugia. Trends Ecol. Evol. 23, 564â571 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d39496e605">6</a></sup>. Although many species recovered at the end of LGM and rapidly expanded north with Holocene climate warming, numerous species became extinct<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Jackson, S. T. &amp; Weng, C. Late quaternary extinction of a tree species in eastern North America. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 96, 13847â13852 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d39496e609">7</a></sup> or failed to recover to pre-LGM population sizes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Willi, Y., Van Buskirk, J. &amp; Hoffmann, A. A. Limits to the adaptive potential of small populations. Ann. Rev. Ecol. Evol. Sys 37, 433â458 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39496e613">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Lynch, M., Conery, J. &amp; Burger, R. Mutation accumulation and the extinction of small populations. Am. Nat. 146, 489â518 (1995)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d39496e616">9</a></sup>. At the same time, human populations expanded rapidly in the Holocene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Ingman, M., Kaessmann, H., Paabo, S. &amp; Gyllensten, U. Mitochondrial genome variation and the origin of modern humans. Nature 408, 708â713 (2000)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d39496e620">10</a></sup> and negatively affected plant and animal recoveries through both hunting and land clearing<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Ruddiman, W. F. Plows, Plagues, and Petroleum: How Humans Took Control of Climate. (Princeton University Press, Princeton, 2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d39496e625">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Ellis, E. C. Ecology in an anthropogenic biosphere. Ecol. Mon. 85, 287â331 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d39496e628">12</a></sup>. Increasing human disturbance and climate warming in the modern era are raising further concerns of accelerated worldwide extinction rates<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Miraldo, A. et al. An Anthropocene map of genetic diversity. Science 353, 1532â1535 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39496e632">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Royal Botanic Gardens (Kew). The State of the Worldâs Plants. (Royal Botanic Gardens, Kew, UK, 2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39496e635">2</a></sup>.</p><p>Studies of the demographic history and extent of the genomic erosion leading to endangerment may both inform management and policy decisions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Garner, B. A. et al. Genomics in conservation: case studies and bridging the gap between data and application. Trends Ecol. Evol. 31, 81â83 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39496e642">13</a></sup> and provide a detailed empirical description of the impact of population collapse on genomic diversity and genetic load<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Allendorf, F. W., Hohenlohe, P. A. &amp; Luikart, G. Genomics and the future of conservation genetics. Nat. Rev. Genet. 11, 697â709 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39496e646">14</a></sup>. In endangered Felide, for instance, population declines predate the beginning of the Holocene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Casas-Marce, M., Soriano, L., Lopez-Bao, J. V. &amp; Godoy, J. A. Genetics at the verge of extinction: insights from the Iberian lynx. Mol. Ecol. 22, 5503â5515 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39496e650">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Dobrynin, P. et al. Genomic legacy of the African cheetah, Acinonyx jubatus. Genome Biol. 16, 277 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d39496e653">16</a></sup>, and these and subsequent bottlenecks have resulted in the accumulation of genetic load and excessive runs of homozygosity that span the genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Robinson, J. A. et al. Genomic flatlining in the endangered island fox. Curr. Biol. 26, 1183â1189 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d39496e657">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Abascal, F. et al. Extreme genomic erosion after recurrent demographic bottlenecks in the highly endangered Iberian lynx. Genome Biol. 17, 251â257 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39496e660">18</a></sup>. Similar patterns of long-term decline are apparent in the genome of Mountain Gorillas, which caused extensive homozygosity and increased genetic load<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Xue, Y. et al. Mountain gorilla genomes reveal the impact of long-term population decline and inbreeding. Science 348, 242â245 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39496e664">19</a></sup>. Paradoxically, this study also revealed a reduction in severely deleterious loss-of-function variants, perhaps resulting from the increased efficacy of purging of deleterious homozygous recessive alleles in small populations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Glemin, S. How are deleterious mutations purged? Drift versus nonrandom mating. Evolution 57, 2678â2687 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d39496e669">20</a></sup>, which may have helped Mountain Gorillas survive at a low population size for thousands of generations. To date, genomic studies of critically endangered species have been conducted on animals, but plants have unique aspects to their life histories that may result in different outcomes as population size declines<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Cronk, Q. Plant extinctions take time. Science 353, 446â447 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39496e673">3</a></sup>. For instance, most plants are capable of self-pollination<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Xue, Y. et al. Mountain gorilla genomes reveal the impact of long-term population decline and inbreeding. Science 348, 242â245 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39496e677">19</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Lande, R. &amp; Schemske, D. W. The evolution of self-fertilization and inbreeding depression in Plants .1. genetic models. Evolution 39, 24â40 (1985)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d39496e680">21</a></sup>, which even at low levels provides a mechanism of systematic inbreeding not present in animals that will strongly contribute to purging of deleterious recessive alleles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Glemin, S. How are deleterious mutations purged? Drift versus nonrandom mating. Evolution 57, 2678â2687 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d39496e684">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hedrick, P. W. Lethals in finite populations. Evolution 56, 654â657 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39496e687">22</a></sup>.</p><p>Trees comprise the dominant elements of the terrestrial landscape and are foundations for ecological stability and longevity in many biomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Petit, R. J., Hu, F. S. &amp; Dick, C. W. Forests of the past: a window to future changes. Science 320, 1450â1453 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39496e694">23</a></sup>. Their extinctions will inevitably lead to additional extinctions of species that rely on trees as a component of their fundamental niche. A total of 1208 trees are currently listed as critically endangered<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Shaw, K., Roy, S. &amp; Wilson, B. Ostrya rehderiana. The IUCN Red List of Threatened Species 2014 e.T32304A2813136 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39496e698">24</a></sup>; however, it remains unknown how these foundation species became endangered<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Miraldo, A. et al. An Anthropocene map of genetic diversity. Science 353, 1532â1535 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39496e702">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Royal Botanic Gardens (Kew). The State of the Worldâs Plants. (Royal Botanic Gardens, Kew, UK, 2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39496e705">2</a></sup> and why they have survived longer than has been predicted<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Cronk, Q. Plant extinctions take time. Science 353, 446â447 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39496e709">3</a></sup>. The most effective way to increase the population size and conserve endangered trees is to directly plant wild-collected seeds or clonally propagate genotypes with stem cuttings<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Falk, D. A. &amp; Holsinger, K. E. Genetics and Conservation of Rare Plants. (Oxford University Press, Demand, 1991)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d39496e713">25</a></sup>, but these strategies require oversight because they may exacerbate inbreeding if parental genomes are not well represented, especially if the source population is extremely small. Here we aim to address these fundamental questions through comparing genomic patterns of diversity between the critically endangered <i>Ostrya rehderiana</i> (IUCN Red List)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Shaw, K., Roy, S. &amp; Wilson, B. Ostrya rehderiana. The IUCN Red List of Threatened Species 2014 e.T32304A2813136 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39496e721">24</a></sup> and the widespread <i>O. chinensis</i>. <i>O. rehderiana</i> is native to southeastern China where rice was first domesticated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Fuller, D. Q. et al. The domestication process and domestication rate in rice: spikelet bases from the Lower Yangtze. Science 323, 1607â1610 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d39496e731">26</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Liu, L. &amp; Chen, X. The Archaeology of China: From the Late Paleolithic to the Early Bronze Age. (Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39496e734">27</a></sup>. This region has been heavily populated by humans for thousands of years. Although seeds from the remaining few large trees have been successfully germinated and grown to maturity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Guan, K. T. Current situation and propagation of rare tree speciesâOstrya rehderiana. J. Zhejiang For. Col. 5, 90â92 (1988)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39496e738">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Yuan, Y. Endangered Mechanism and Protective Measures of Ostrya rehderiana Chun. Dissertation for the Degree of Master. Zhejiang Agriculture and Forestry University, Zhejiang (2015) (in Chinese with English abstract)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d39496e741">29</a></sup>, wild populations may soon be extinct. The close relative <i>O. chinensis</i> (=<i>O. multinervis</i>), however, has relatively large wild populations, which are distributed from southeastern China to the high mountains of southwestern China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Lu, Z. et al. Species delimitation of Chinese hop-hornbeams based on molecular and morphological evidence. Ecol. Evol. 6, 4731â440 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d39496e752">30</a></sup>, an area that was only recently colonized by humans and remains sparsely populated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Fitzgerald, C. P. The Southward expansion of Chinese People (Australian National University Press, Canberra, 1972)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d39496e756">31</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Lee, J. The legacy of immigration in Southwest China, 1250â1850. Ann. Demogr. Histor. 1, 279â304 (PersÃ©e-Portail des revues scientifiques en SHS, 1982)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d39496e759">32</a></sup>. Wood produced by both species is extremely hard and is highly prized for construction of boats and religious temples<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Cheng, J., Yang, J. &amp; Liu, P. Timbers in China. (China Forestry Publishing House, Beijing, 1992)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d39496e763">33</a></sup>. Both of these species are deciduous, monecious, primarily outcrossing, and rarely reproduce clonally<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Li, P. C. &amp; Skvortsov, A. K. Betulaceae. In Flora of China (eds. Wu, C. Y. &amp; Raven, P. H.) 286â313 (Science Press, Beijing; Missouri Botanical Garden Press, St. Louis, 1999)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d39496e767">34</a></sup>, so factors impacting historical fluctuations in population size likely are associated more with extrinsic factors associated with the locations of their ranges than differences in life history.</p><p>We sequenced and assembled de novo genomes of both <i>O. chinensis</i> and <i>O. rehderiana</i> and re-sequenced 13 additional individuals of each species for population genomic analyses. Today only five <i>O. rehderiana</i> very old individuals (&gt;100 years old) currently reside in a single wild population, and about 30 years ago, ~300 <i>O. rehderiana</i> trees were planted from successfully germinated seeds intentionally collected from the old stand, which at that time was comprised of six or seven mature trees<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Guan, K. T. Current situation and propagation of rare tree speciesâOstrya rehderiana. J. Zhejiang For. Col. 5, 90â92 (1988)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39496e786">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Yuan, Y. Endangered Mechanism and Protective Measures of Ostrya rehderiana Chun. Dissertation for the Degree of Master. Zhejiang Agriculture and Forestry University, Zhejiang (2015) (in Chinese with English abstract)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d39496e789">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Jin, S. H., Ding, B. Y., Yu, M. J. &amp; Jiang, W. M. Current situation of distribution and conservation of national wild plants for protection in Zhejiang Province. J. Zhejiang For. Sci. Technol. 22, 48â53 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d39496e792">35</a></sup>. Comparisons between the old and young <i>O. rehderiana</i> trees allowed us to contrast the very recent impacts of inbreeding on genomic diversity to longer-scale demographic impacts. Based on these genomic data, we addressed the following questions: (1) do these two species show similar demographic histories in response to the Quaternary climate change? If not, when did their demographics begin to diverge? (2) Have deleterious variations accumulated in the endangered species at a greater rate than in the widespread tree, and have they impacted the potential to recover? and (3) have more highly recessive deleterious variations been purged by drift in the endangered species? The answers to these questions will aid in identifying plant genomes that are on the cusp of demographic collapse.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assemblies and annotations</h3><p>De novo genomes of one 300-year-old <i>O. rehderiana</i> individual and one <i>O. chinensis</i> individual (&gt;20 years old) from wild populations were sequenced to 128Ã and 340Ã depth of coverage (based on an estimated genome size of ~386âMb), respectively (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">1</a>, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">2</a>). The assembled genome sequences were 366.2âMb (scaffold N50 of 2.31âMb; contig N50 of 21.96âkb) in <i>O. rehderiana</i> and 371.6âMb (scaffold N50 of 0.81âMb; contig N50 of 13.65âkb) in <i>O. chinensis</i> with a high contiguity, coverage, and accuracy (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig1">1</a>, TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary noteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">1</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">2</a>, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">3</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">7</a>). In addition, both genomes contained more than 51% repetitive elements, and a total of 27,831 and 31,152 protein-coding genes were predicted in the <i>O. rehderiana</i> and <i>O. chinensis</i> genomes, respectively (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig1">1</a>, TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Tab1">1</a>, Supplementary noteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">1</a>, Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">4</a>, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">8</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">13</a>). Neither species has undergone a recent whole genome duplication (WGD; Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">2</a>, Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">6</a>), as found for <i>Betula pendula</i>, from another genus within the Betulaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Salojarvi, J. et al. Genome sequencing and population genomic analyses provide insights into the adaptive landscape of silver birch. Nat. Genet. 49, 904â908 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d39496e894">36</a></sup>, and the divergence time between the two ironwood species was estimated at ~6.95 (3.4â12.9) million years ago (Mya) based on the fossil-calibrated phylogeny (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">7</a>). A total of 243 unique and 526 expanded gene families were present in <i>O. rehderiana</i>, whereas 434 unique and 880 expanded gene families were present in <i>O. chinensis</i> (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">8</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">9</a>, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">14</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">17</a>). We also identified 590 gene families that expanded in the ancestral lineage of the two ironwood species with predicted functions enriched in lignin catabolism, hydrolase activity, and Î²-galactosidase activity compared with silver birch (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">9</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">18</a>). Of these gene families, 43 were related to wood formation (Supplementary noteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">3</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">19</a>). For example, the expanded fasciclin-like arabinogalactan protein gene family (FLAs, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">10</a>) are critical for regulating stem strength and stiffness by affecting the molecular composition and architecture of the secondary cell wall<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="MacMillan, C. P., Mansfield, S. D., Stachurski, Z. H., Evans, R. &amp; Southerton, S. G. Fasciclin-like arabinogalactan proteins: specialization for stem biomechanics and cell wall architecture in Arabidopsis and Eucalyptus. Plant J. 62, 689â703 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d39496e936">37</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-07913-4/MediaObjects/41467_2018_7913_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-07913-4/MediaObjects/41467_2018_7913_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="697" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>An overview of <i>O. rehderiana</i> (blue), <i>O. chinensis</i> (green), and <i>B. pendula</i> (purple) genomes. Different tracks denote (moving outwards): (I) synteny blocks between <i>O. rehderiana</i> and <i>O. chinensis</i> (green), <i>O. rehderiana</i> and <i>B. pendula</i> (blue), and <i>O. chinensis</i> and <i>B. pendula</i> (green); (II) GC content (0.2â0.8); (III) repeat density at 10âkb (0â1, from white to dark blue); and (IV) gene density in 10âkb (0â30, from white to purple). Links in the core connect syntenous genes (MCscanX results)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly and annotation features of two genomes</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Demographic histories</h3><p>Based on re-sequencing data from the assembled genome, four additional large (&gt;100 years old), and nine additional younger <i>O. rehderiana</i> trees (&lt;30 years old, which were planted from seeds from up to seven large parental trees with one to two of them dead in the 1990s), and 13 additional large <i>O. chinensis</i> trees (&gt;20 years old; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">11</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">20</a>), both species exhibited high <i>N</i><sub>e</sub>â~â1.3 Mya followed by two sharp declines in effective population size (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig2">2</a>, the same demographic trends were found when only the older <i>O. rehderiana</i> trees were analyzed; Supplementary noteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">4</a>, Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">13</a>, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">21</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">22</a>). The first decline occurred from 1.2 to 0.4âMya, which coincided with the decline of the atmospheric surface air temperature (Tsurf), the escalation of the Chinese loess mass accumulation rate (MAR)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Sun, Y. &amp; An, Z. Late PlioceneâPleistocene changes in mass accumulation rates of eolian deposits on the central Chinese Loess Plateau. J. Geophy. Res. Atmos. 110, D23101 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1349">38</a></sup>, and the development Naynayxungla glaciation (0.8â0.50âMya), the largest in the Qinghai-Tibet Plateau<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zheng, B., Xu, Q. &amp; Shen, Y. The relationship between climate change and Quaternary glacial cycles on the QinghaiâTibetan Plateau: review and speculation. Quat. Int. 97, 93â101 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1353">39</a></sup> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig2">2</a>). The second decline occurred between 40,000 and 8000 years ago, and was initiated during the development of the LGM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Clark, P. U. et al. The Last Glacial Maximum. Science 325, 710â714 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1360">40</a></sup>. Following the end of LGM, however, the <i>N</i><sub>e</sub> of <i>O. rehderiana</i> continued to decline to near zero, while the population size of <i>O. chinensis</i> expanded in the middle Holocene (~5000 years before the present), coincident with increased temperature and precipitation in China<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Wang, Y. et al. The Holocene Asian monsoon: links to solar changes and North Atlantic climate. Science 308, 854â857 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1375">41</a></sup>. In contrast to <i>O. rehderiana</i>, <i>O. chinensis</i> maintained a stable population size throughout the remaining Holocene and currently is not endangered.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-07913-4/MediaObjects/41467_2018_7913_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-018-07913-4/MediaObjects/41467_2018_7913_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="479" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Demographic histories of <i>O. rehderiana</i> and <i>O. chinensis</i>. Shown are MSMC estimates of the effective population size (<i>N</i><sub>e</sub>) for <i>O. rehderiana</i> (blue, using Ore01âOre02âOre03âOre04) and <i>O. chinensis</i> (green, using Och01âOch05âOch06âOch10) over the last 3 million years based on eight phased haplotypes in each species. The time scale on the <i>x-</i>axis is calculated assuming a mutation rate per generation of (<i>Î¼</i>)â=â2.182âÃâ10<sup>â8</sup> and a generation time of (<i>g</i>)â=â10 years. The last glacial maximum (LGM) and the Naynayxungla glaciation are highlighted in gray vertical bars. MARâ=âloess mass accumulation rate; Tsurfâ=âatmospheric surface air temperature</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Accumulation of deleterious variations in <i>O. rehderiana</i>
                        </h3><p><i>O. rehderiana</i> displayed extremely low observed sequence diversity (<i>Ï</i>â=â1.66Eâ3, 95% CI: 1.65Eâ3â ââ1.67Eâ3) compared to <i>O. chinensis</i> (<i>Ï</i>â=â2.79Eâ03; 95% CI: 2.77Eâ3âââ2.81Eâ3), <i>Betula pendula</i> (<i>Ï</i>â=â8.84Eâ3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Salojarvi, J. et al. Genome sequencing and population genomic analyses provide insights into the adaptive landscape of silver birch. Nat. Genet. 49, 904â908 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1462">36</a></sup>, and most other trees (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig3">3b</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">14</a>, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">23</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">24</a>), and is similar to <i>Prunus persica</i>, whose genome-wide diversity has been severely restricted by domestication<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="International Peach Genome, I. et al. The high-quality draft genome of peach (Prunus persica) identifies unique patterns of genetic diversity, domestication and genome evolution. Nat. Genet. 45, 487â494 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1482">42</a></sup>. The average observed heterozygosity and numbers of the single nucleotide variants (SNVs) across intergenic regions, introns, and coding regions were significantly lower for <i>O. rehderiana</i> than <i>O. chinensis</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig4">4a</a>, Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">25</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">26</a>). Estimates for heterozygosity in sub-samples from individuals with high sequence depth also exhibited similar lower heterozygosity in <i>O. rehderiana</i> than in <i>O. chinensis</i>, indicating that the effects of potential artifacts due to different sequencing depths were minimal (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">27</a>). Among the sampled <i>O. rehderiana</i> trees, the observed heterozygosity exhibited a negative correlation with the estimated tree ages (slopeâ=ââ4.1eâ6, <i>r</i><sup>2</sup>â=â0.68; Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig3">3c</a>), indicating that heterozygosity in the younger trees planted for conservation is low compared to the older trees and is consistent with the high relatedness among extant individuals (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">15</a>). Additional patterns that are consistent with the extreme population contraction of <i>O. rehderiana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Wakeley, J. Coalescent Theory. An Introduction (Roberts and Company Publishers, Greenwood Village, 2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1527">43</a></sup> include a more uniform site frequency spectrum (SFS), higher frequencies of derived alleles, and an elevated Tajimaâs <i>D</i> when compared to <i>O. chinensis</i> (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">16</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">18</a>). Inbreeding, as estimated by the frequency of runs of homozygosity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="McQuillan, R. et al. Runs of homozygosity in European populations. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 83, 359â372 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1544">44</a></sup> (FROH; sum of ROHâ&gt;â100âkb/genome effective length), was higher in <i>O. rehderiana</i> (FROH range 0.31â0.45) than in <i>O. chinensis</i> (FROH range 0.07â0.19; Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig4">4b</a>); every <i>O. rehderiana</i> individual exhibited several ROHâ&gt;â1âMb, whereas the longest ROH in <i>O. chinensis</i> wasâ&lt;0.63âMb (Supplementary noteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">5</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">19</a>). Importantly, patterns of genomic diversity indicated that the nine young trees (&lt;30 years old) of <i>O. rehderiana</i> were derived from only one common maternal and more than two paternal parents, which exacerbated the low levels of population genomic diversity and increased inbreeding (Supplementary noteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">6</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">20</a>). Finally, <i>O. rehderiana</i> had slow linkage disequilibrium (LD) decay. Half the maximum <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> was not attained until ~444âkb, whereas half the maximum <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> for <i>O. chinensis</i> was attained at ~29âkb (the difference between species was not affected by including the young <i>O. rehderiana</i>, Supplementary noteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">6</a>, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig4">4c</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">21</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">28</a>). A negative correlation between the population recombination rate (<i>Ï</i>) and the number of deleterious mutations, was detected in both <i>O. rehderiana</i> and <i>O. chinesis</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">22</a>), a finding consistent with previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Zhang, M., Zhou, L., Bawa, R., Suren, H. &amp; Holliday, J. A. Recombination rate variation, hitchhiking, and demographic history shape deleterious load in poplar. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 2899â2910 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1623">45</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Kono, T. J. et al. The role of deleterious substitutions in crop genomes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 33, 2307â2317 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1626">46</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp"
