/></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Diversity, linkage disequilibrium, and genetic load metrics for <i>O. rehderiana</i> and <i>O. chinensis</i>. <b>a</b> Bar chart of heterozygosity of the whole genome and intergenic, intron, and coding regions for each individual in the two species. Error bars represent two-fold standard deviations. <b>b</b> Box plot of FROH for each individual in the two species. The line in the center of the box represent the median values, the edges of the box represent the first and third quartiles, and the whiskers above and below the box show the range of values. <b>c</b> Linkage disequilibrium (LD) decay of the <i>O. rehderiana</i> (Ore01âOre04) and <i>O. chinensis</i> (Och01âOch14) genomes. Open circles denote distances where the <i>r</i><sup>2</sup> correlation coefficient reduces to half of its maximum (~444âkb for <i>O. rehderiana</i> and ~29âkb for <i>O. chinensis</i>). <b>d</b> The negative relationship between neutral (intergenic) diversity and the ratio of heterozygosity at zero-fold relative to four-fold sites. The triangle represents <i>O. rehderiana</i> individuals and the square represent <i>O. chinensis</i> individuals. <b>e</b> A Venn diagram of the deleterious variants predicted by PolyPhen-2, PROVEAN, and SIFT. <b>f</b> Comparison of deleterious genetic variation in <i>O. rehderiana</i> and <i>O. chinensis</i>. The total number of derived alleles is based on counting each heterozygous genotype once and each homozygous-derived genotype twice</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Several patterns indicated consistently weaker purifying selection and greater accumulation of genetic load in <i>O. rehderiana</i> compared to <i>O. chinensis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Petit, R. J., Hu, F. S. &amp; Dick, C. W. Forests of the past: a window to future changes. Science 320, 1450â1453 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1766">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Marsden, C. D. et al. Bottlenecks and selective sweeps during domestication have increased deleterious genetic variation in dogs. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 152â157 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1769">47</a></sup>. The ratio of the heterozygosity of zero-fold to four-fold degenerate sites showed a negative relationship with neutral heterozygosity and was significantly elevated in <i>O</i>. <i>rehderiana</i> compared to <i>O. chinensis</i> (<i>T</i>-test, <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.02, Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig4">4d</a>). The site frequency distribution for derived deleterious variants, as identified by PolyPhen2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Adzhubei, I. A. et al. A method and server for predicting damaging missense mutations. Nat. Methods 7, 248â249 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1792">48</a></sup>, PROVEAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Choi, Y., Sims, G. E., Murphy, S., Miller, J. R. &amp; Chan, A. P. Predicting the functional effect of amino acid substitutions and indels. PLoS One 7, e46688 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1796">49</a></sup>, and SIFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Ng, P. C. &amp; Henikoff, S. SIFT: predicting amino acid changes that affect protein function. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 3812â3814 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1800">50</a></sup>, was more uniformly distributed across frequency classes in <i>O. rehderiana</i> than in <i>O. chinesis</i>, and the rare frequency classes were enriched for deleterious (DEL) and tolerated (TOL) sites compared to synonymous (SYN) sites in <i>O. chinensis</i>, but not in <i>O. rehderiana</i> (Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">16</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">17</a>), which were both indicative of weaker purifying selection in the critically endangered species. To further explore the patterns in genetic load, we estimated the proportion of the heterozygous, homozygous ancestral, and homozygous-derived mutations within four categories: SYN, TOL, DEL, and loss of function (LoF) among all samples trees (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig4">4f</a>) and using only the five old <i>O. rehderiana</i> trees (results were consistent throughout; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">23</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">29</a>). Consistent with the higher relatedness in the extant <i>O. rehderiana</i> trees, observed homozygosity was higher in <i>O. rehderiana</i> than in <i>O. chinensis</i> for SYN, TOL, and DEL variants (all <i>P</i> values were &lt;0.05, MannâWhitney <i>U</i>-test). In <i>O. rehderiana</i>, an average of 5350 DEL variants were homozygous across 3793 genes in each individual, whereas in <i>O. chinensis</i> 4359 DEL variants were homozygous across 3793 genes in each individual (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">24</a>, Supplementary Data filesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM4">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM5">2</a>). In total, <i>O. rehderiana</i> individuals carried ~104 more derived DEL deleterious alleles than <i>O. chinensis</i>. For the LoF variants, however, <i>O. rehderiana</i> individuals carried proportionally fewer derived homozygous LoF variants than <i>O. chinensis</i> individuals (~501 homozygous LoF variants in ~485 genes for <i>O. rehderiana</i> vs. ~770 in ~691 genes for <i>O. chinensis</i>; Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig4">4f</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">24</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">29</a>). GO annotations for DEL and LoF mutations were enriched in the Molecular Functions category (Supplementary noteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">30</a>).</p><p>In the nine young trees, the distribution of all four categories of sites (SYN, TOL, DEL, LoF) showed a lower number of heterozygous-derived sites, a slightly higher number of homozygous sites, and a similar number of total derived alleles compared to the five old trees, but not all comparisons were statistically significant (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">20d</a>). To address whether the increased genetic load in the young <i>O. rehderiana</i> trees had detrimental effects on fitness, we counted the numbers of developed cymules after pollination (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">8</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">25</a>). We found that the young trees produced fewer developed cymules per catkin than the old trees (nested ANOVA, <i>F</i><sub>1,8</sub>â=â11.3, <i>P</i>â&lt;â0.01), suggesting that reproductive fitness was reduced in the younger trees as expected from inbreeding depression (Supplementary noteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">8</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">26</a>, Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">31</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec6" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec6-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec6">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec6-content"><p>The population collapse of <i>O. rehderiana</i> was likely caused by a combination of historical climate change and anthropogenic disturbance. The population size decline in <i>O. rehderiana</i> began ~1âMya, just prior to the Naynayxungla glaciation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Zheng, B., Xu, Q. &amp; Shen, Y. The relationship between climate change and Quaternary glacial cycles on the QinghaiâTibetan Plateau: review and speculation. Quat. Int. 97, 93â101 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1950">39</a></sup>, coincident with a reduction in <i>O. chinensis</i>, but unlike <i>O. chinensis</i> the population size of <i>O. rehderiana</i> never stabilized and kept falling through the LGM and the Holocene (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig2">2</a>). Two factors might account for the continued decline in population size of <i>O. rehderiana</i> after the LGM. First, at some point after the LGM the <i>N</i><sub>e</sub> of <i>O. rehderiana</i> may have decreased to a threshold size that constrained recovery and caused it to enter into an extinction vortex<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Hutchings, J. A. Thresholds for impaired species recovery. Proc. Biol. Sci. 282, 20150654 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1978">51</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Gilpin, M. Minimum viable populations: processes of extinction. In Conservation Biology: The Science of Scarcity and Diversity (ed. SoulÃ©, M. E.) (Sinauer Associates, Sunderland, MA, 1986)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1981">52</a></sup>. The low level of genetic diversity may have inhibited the adaptive potential of populations by undermining their ability to adapt to new edaphic and photoperiodic environments during migration<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Allendorf, F. W., Hohenlohe, P. A. &amp; Luikart, G. Genomics and the future of conservation genetics. Nat. Rev. Genet. 11, 697â709 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1985">14</a></sup> or respond to pathogens under the warming habitats of the Holocene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Huntley, B. The dynamic response of plants to environmental change and the resulting risks of extinction. In Conservation in a Changing World (eds. Mace, G. M., Balmford, A. &amp; Ginsberg, J. R.) 69â85 (Cambridge: Cambrigde University Press, 1999)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1989">53</a></sup>. Second, in the Holocene humans directly diminished <i>O. rehderiana</i> population sizes by cutting trees for construction and clearing land for rice farming<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Shaw, K., Roy, S. &amp; Wilson, B. Ostrya rehderiana. The IUCN Red List of Threatened Species 2014 e.T32304A2813136 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1996">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Liu, L. &amp; Chen, X. The Archaeology of China: From the Late Paleolithic to the Early Bronze Age. (Cambridge University Press, Cambridge, 2012)." href="#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1999">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Guan, K. T. Current situation and propagation of rare tree speciesâOstrya rehderiana. J. Zhejiang For. Col. 5, 90â92 (1988)." href="#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39496e1999_1">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Yuan, Y. Endangered Mechanism and Protective Measures of Ostrya rehderiana Chun. Dissertation for the Degree of Master. Zhejiang Agriculture and Forestry University, Zhejiang (2015) (in Chinese with English abstract)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2002">29</a></sup>. Along with low effective population size and impacts of genetic load, pressures from large human populations in eastern China may have tipped the balance, leading to populations that were unable to recover. Attribution of the accurate cause of the endangered status of <i>O. rehderiana</i>, however, is difficult in the absence of information on the past geographic distribution and detailed paleobotanical data.</p><p>Our study further revealed the effects of the population size decline on genome-level patterns of genetic diversity in this long-lived outcrossing tree. Trees are unusual among the angiosperms because selfing and mixed-mating breeding systems are rare compared to other angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Olson, M. S., Hamrick, J. &amp; Moore, R. Breeding systems, mating systems, and genomics of gender determination in angiosperm trees. In Comparative and Evolutionary Genomics of Angiosperm Trees. Plant Genetics and Genomics: Crops and Models, Vol. 21 (eds. Groover A. &amp; Cronk Q.) (Springer Press, Cham, 2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2012">54</a></sup>, suggesting that inbreeding is particularly harmful in its immediate impacts on tree populations. <i>O. rehderiana</i> exhibits high levels of inbreeding, with two of the five extant old individuals likely being monozygotic twins (Ore04 and Ore05). Although our sample size for young trees was small (<i>n</i>â=â9), all of them were half-sibs, indicating that without intervention, future kin-mating among these planted trees could further enhance the inbreeding bottleneck of this endangered tree. Future management efforts should focus on reducing inbreeding, which may have already led to a decrease in female cymule development (Supplementary noteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">8</a>), a likely decrease in seed set and an increase in mortality<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Guan, K. T. Current situation and propagation of rare tree speciesâOstrya rehderiana. J. Zhejiang For. Col. 5, 90â92 (1988)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2025">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Yuan, Y. Endangered Mechanism and Protective Measures of Ostrya rehderiana Chun. Dissertation for the Degree of Master. Zhejiang Agriculture and Forestry University, Zhejiang (2015) (in Chinese with English abstract)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2028">29</a></sup>. The observed high levels of relatedness and inbreeding among the outcrossing <i>O. rehderiana</i> trees, generated patterns of LD, runs of homozygosity, and reduced diversity similarly observed in critically endangered animals<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Provan, J. &amp; Bennett, K. Phylogeographic insights into cryptic glacial refugia. Trends Ecol. Evol. 23, 564â571 (2008)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2036">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jackson, S. T. &amp; Weng, C. Late quaternary extinction of a tree species in eastern North America. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 96, 13847â13852 (1999)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2036_1">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Willi, Y., Van Buskirk, J. &amp; Hoffmann, A. A. Limits to the adaptive potential of small populations. Ann. Rev. Ecol. Evol. Sys 37, 433â458 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2039">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Petit, R. J., Hu, F. S. &amp; Dick, C. W. Forests of the past: a window to future changes. Science 320, 1450â1453 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2042">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Li, S. et al. Genomic signatures of near-extinction and rebirth of the crested ibis and other endangered bird species. Genome Biol. 15, 557 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2045">55</a></sup>. In particular, genetic drift in the small populations of <i>O. rehderiana</i> has noticeably reduced the strength of purifying selection, allowing alleles with deleterious effects to persist and fix in the population and resulting in higher genetic loads than in <i>O. chinensis</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig4">4</a>). Similar patterns have been found in other species with dramatically reduced population sizes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="DeSalle, R. Genetics at the brink of extinction. Heredity 94, 386â387 (2005)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2058">56</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Doniger, S. W. et al. A catalog of neutral and deleterious polymorphism in yeast. PLoS Genet. 4, e1000183 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2061">57</a></sup>.</p><p>In contrast to the high genetic load and reduced fitness observed in <i>O. rehderiana</i>, we found fewer LoF variants than in <i>O. chinensis</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig4">4f</a>), possibly resulting from more effective purging of the highly deleterious variants in the extremely small populations of <i>O. rehderiana</i>. This is analogous to the proverb, âthe extremity reached, the course reversedâ. Purging of the highly deleterious variants may have resulted in a gradual reduction in inbreeding depression during its long-protracted population decline (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#Fig2">2</a>), which may have allowed this species to survive at low population sizes over extended time periods and may contribute to its future survival, if anthropogenic disturbance can be eliminated.</p><p>Our genomic investigation of <i>O. rehderiana</i> provides an example of the pattern of genetic diversity erosion in long-lived trees and what may be a genetic mechanism for the provisional survival of such endangered species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Cronk, Q. Plant extinctions take time. Science 353, 446â447 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2089">3</a></sup>. Although extinctions of some endangered plants lag behind the predicted date of extinction, they âmay already be functionally extinctâ<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Davis, M. B. &amp; Shaw, R. G. Range shifts and adaptive responses to Quaternary climate change. Science 292, 673â679 (2001)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2093">5</a></sup>. Therefore, many of the 1208 critically endangered trees on the IUCN Red list<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Shaw, K., Roy, S. &amp; Wilson, B. Ostrya rehderiana. The IUCN Red List of Threatened Species 2014 e.T32304A2813136 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2097">24</a></sup> may require unique interventions to increase both their census and effective population sizes. Future efforts should be focused on designing artificial crossing strategies to reduce inbred progenies and the loss of diversity through genetic drift, rather than increasing the total number of the surviving individuals through the collection of inbred seeds or clonal cuttings in endangered trees<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Falk, D. A. &amp; Holsinger, K. E. Genetics and Conservation of Rare Plants. (Oxford University Press, Demand, 1991)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2101">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Whitmore, T. C., Sayer, J. A. (eds.). Tropical Deforestation and Species Extinction (Chapman Hall, London, UK, 1992)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2104">58</a></sup>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec7" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec7-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec7">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec7-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Source and sequencing of genomic DNA</h3><p>High molecular weight genomic DNA was extracted from mature leaves of 14 <i>O. rehderiana</i> (five large treesââ¥â100 years old, Ore01âOre05 and nine young trees ~30 years old, Ore06âOre14), 14 <i>O. chinensis</i> (synonym to <i>O. multinervis</i>), two <i>C. cordata</i>, one <i>C. fangiana</i>, and one <i>Ostryopsis nobilis</i> individual using the CTAB method<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Tel-Zur, N., Abbo, S., Myslabodski, D. &amp; Mizrahi, Y. Modified CTAB procedure for DNA isolation from epiphytic cacti of the genera Hylocereus and Selenicereus (Cactaceae). Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 17, 249â254 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2139">59</a></sup>. One large <i>O. rehderiana</i> individual and one <i>O. chinensis</i> individual located in Tianmu Mountain, Zhejiang and Zehei Township, Yunnan were selected for assembling de novo genomes, respectively. High coverage shotgun-sequencing was conducted for the Illumina HiSeq2500 platform with the short, medium and long insert libraries. Statistics for the obtained reads are given in Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">1</a>. The additional 13 samples for each species were re-sequenced at medium coverage (9â30Ã) using 500âbp insert size libraries (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">20</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">RNA sequencing</h3><p>Total RNAs from four tissues (roots, leaves, phloem, and xylem) were collected from the wild <i>O. rehderiana</i> and extracted using a CTAB procedure<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Tel-Zur, N., Abbo, S., Myslabodski, D. &amp; Mizrahi, Y. Modified CTAB procedure for DNA isolation from epiphytic cacti of the genera Hylocereus and Selenicereus (Cactaceae). Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. 17, 249â254 (1999)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2166">59</a></sup> for transcriptome sequencing. cDNA libraries with insert sizes of 200âbp were constructed, and sequencing was conducted using the Illumina Genome Analyzer platform. After trimming adaptor sequences and filtering out low-quality reads, the RNA data was assembled using Trinity v2.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Grabherr, M. G. et al. Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat. Biotechnol. 29, 644 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2170">60</a></sup> for all reads combined, and for each of the four tissues separately (SeeÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">supplement methods</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Genome assembly</h3><p>Before assembly, we filtered the low-quality sequencing reads, including removing adaptor sequences by SCYTHE (<a href="https://github.com/vsbuffalo/scythe">https://github.com/vsbuffalo/scythe</a>) and trimming low-quality sequences using SICKLE (<a href="https://github.com/najoshi/sickle">https://github.com/najoshi/sickle</a>). We employed SOAPec<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2199">61</a></sup> to minimize the influence of sequencing errors with low-frequency cutoff of 4. A total of 49.56G (or 128.39Ã) and 133.36G (or 345.48Ã) of data were retained for assembly of the <i>O. rehderiana</i> and <i>O. chinensis</i> genomes, respectively. The two <i>Ostrya</i> genomes were assembled de novo using Platanus v1.2.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Kajitani, R. et al. Efficient de novo assembly of highly heterozygous genomes from whole-genome shotgun short reads. Genome Res. 24, 1384â1395 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2213">62</a></sup>, which is optimized for high-throughput Illumina sequence data and heterozygous diploid genomes. Briefly, Platanus assembles reads through three modules: <i>de Bruijn</i> graph-based contig assembly, scaffolding, and gap closing. The final assemblies were polished by GapCloser<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2220">61</a></sup> to further close the gaps in the Platanus assemblies.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Gene prediction</h3><p>Homology-based and de novo methods were used to predict genes in <i>O. chinensis</i> genome, whereas in <i>O. rehderiana</i> genome we also used our RNA-seq data. Integrated gene sets were generated by EVidenceModeler (EVM)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Automated eukaryotic gene structure annotation using EVidenceModeler and the Program to Assemble Spliced Alignments. Genome Biol. 9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2238">63</a></sup> and functional assignments for all genes were generated by aligning their CDS to sequences available in the public protein databases including KEGG, SwissProt, TrEMBL, and InterProScan (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">13</a>). The noncoding RNAs were also identified by the de novo approach based on the specific structures and the homology of the known databases (SeeÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">supplement methods</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Gene family clusters</h3><p>All protein sequences from eight species (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana, Carica papaya, Juglans regia, Fragaria vesca, Oryza sativa</i> ssp. <i>Japonica, P. persica, Ricinus communis, Vitis vinifera</i>) from NCBI were used to generate clusters of gene families (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">32</a>). Gene sets were filtered by selecting the longest ORF for each gene. ORFs with premature stop codons, not multiples of 3 in length, or fewer than 50 amino acids were removed. Gene families were constructed using the OrthoMCL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C. J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D. S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2266">64</a></sup> method on the all-vs.-all <i>BLASTP</i> (<i>E</i>-valueââ¤â1eâ5) alignments. 1896 single-copy genes were identified within the 10 species, and subsequently used to build a phylogenetic tree. Coding DNA sequence (CDS) alignments of each single-copy family were created based on the protein alignment, using MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792â1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2277">65</a></sup> software. The phylogenetic tree was reconstructed with PhyML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Guindon, S. et al. New algorithms and methods to estimate maximum-likelihood phylogenies: assessing the performance of PhyML 3.0. Syst. Biol. 59, 307â321 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2281">66</a></sup> software under the GTRâ+âgamma model using only four-fold degenerate sites, which are less likely to be influenced by selection and more likely to evolve in a manner consistent with a molecular clock. To estimate divergence times, the approximate likelihood calculations were conducted using the PAML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2285">67</a></sup> mcmctree program assuming a correlated molecular clock model and a REV substitution model. After a burn-in of 5,000,000 iterations, the MCMC process was performed 20,000 times with sample frequency of 5000. Convergence was checked by Tracer v1.4 (<a href="http://beast.community/tracer">http://beast.community/tracer</a>) and confirmed by two independent runs. The following constraints were used for time calibrations:</p><ol class="u-list-style-none">
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">1.</span>
                      
                        <p>140â150 Mya for the monocotâdicot split<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Gaut, B. S., Morton, B. R., McCaig, B. C. &amp; Clegg, M. T. Substitution rate comparisons between grasses and palms: synonymous rate differences at the nuclear gene Adh parallel rate differences at the plastid gene rbcL. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 93, 10274â10279 (1996)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2306">68</a></sup>,</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">2.</span>
                      
                        <p>94 Mya as the lower boundary for the <i>Vitis</i>âEurosid split<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Nystedt, B. et al. The Norway spruce genome sequence and conifer genome evolution. Nature 497, 579â584 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2324">69</a></sup>,</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">3.</span>
                      
                        <p>54â90 Mya for <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>C. papaya</i> split (<a href="http://www.timetree.org">http://www.timetree.org</a>),</p>
                      
                    </li>
                    <li>
                      <span class="u-custom-list-number">4.</span>
                      
                        <p>90â106 Mya for <i>P. persica</i> and <i>J. regia</i> split (<a href="http://www.timetree.org">http://www.timetree.org</a>).</p>
                      
                    </li>
                  </ol><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Gene family comparison and expansion analysis</h3><p>To study gene gain and loss, Computational Analysis of gene Family Evolution (CAFÃ)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="De Bie, T., Cristianini, N., Demuth, J. P. &amp; Hahn, M. W. CAFE: a computational tool for the study of gene family evolution. Bioinformatics 22, 1269â1271 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2387">70</a></sup> software was applied to estimate the universal gene birth and death rate <i>Î»</i> (lambda) under a random birth and death model for each branch of the phylogenetic tree using a maximum likelihood method. In addition, GO and KEGG enrichment for genes in gene families that expanded and contracted in <i>O. rehderiana, O. chinensis</i>, and their ancestor lineage were also calculated using GOEAST<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Zheng, Q. &amp; Wang, X. J. GOEAST: a web-based software toolkit for Gene Ontology enrichment analysis. Nucleic Acids Res. 36, W358âW363 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2397">71</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Resequencing reads mapping</h3><p>Thirteen <i>O. rehderiana</i> and 13Â <i>O. chinensis</i> individuals were selected for whole genome resequencing. Adapter sequences were trimmed from the raw reads and low-quality sequences (quality scoreâ&lt;â20) were filtered using SCYTHE (<a href="https://github.com/vsbuffalo/scythe">https://github.com/vsbuffalo/scythe</a>) and SICKLE (<a href="https://github.com/najoshi/sickle">https://github.com/najoshi/sickle</a>). Filtered reads were mapped to either the <i>O. rehderiana</i> or <i>O. chinensis</i> genome (scaffolds lengthâ&gt;â2âkb) by BWA-MEM software with default parameters. Sequence alignment/map (SAM) format files were imported to SAMtools v0.1.19<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2436">72</a></sup> for sorting and merging, and Picard v1.92 (<a href="http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard">http://broadinstitute.github.io/picard</a>) was used to assign read group information containing library, lane, and sample identity. The Genome Analysis Toolkit (GATK, v3.6)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="DePristo, M. A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491â498 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2447">73</a></sup> was used to perform local realignment of reads to enhance the alignments near indel polymorphisms in two steps. The first step used the RealignerTargetCreator to identify regions where realignment was needed, and the second step used IndelRealigner to realign the regions found in the first step, which generated a realigned binary sequence alignment/map (BAM) file for each individual.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">SNP and genotype calling</h3><p>Single-sample SNP and genotype calling were implemented in GATK with HaplotypeCaller<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="DePristo, M. A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491â498 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2459">73</a></sup> to prevent biases in SNP calling accuracy between groups with different numbers of samples. For single-sample SNP and genotype calling, a number of filtering steps were performed to reduce false positives, including removal of (1) indels with a quality scores &lt;30, (2) SNPs with more than two alleles, (3) SNPs at or within 5âbp from any indels, (4) SNPs with a genotyping quality scores (GQ) &lt;10, and (5) SNPs with extremely low (&lt;one-third average depth) or extremely high (&gt;threefold average depth) coverage. Multi-sample SNPs were identified after merging the results of each individual by GenotypeGVCFs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="DePristo, M. A. et al. A framework for variation discovery and genotyping using next-generation DNA sequencing data. Nat. Genet. 43, 491â498 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2463">73</a></sup> to generate three datasets: dataset 1, SNPs from all <i>O. rehderiana</i> samples called using the <i>O. rehderiana</i> genome as reference; dataset 2, SNPs from all <i>O. chinensis</i> samples called using the <i>O. chinensis</i> genome as reference; and dataset 3, SNPs from all <i>O. rehderiana</i>, <i>O. chinensis</i>, <i>C. cordata</i>, <i>C. fangiana</i>, <i>O. nobilis</i> samples and using the <i>O. rehderiana</i> genome as reference. Each multi-sample SNP was first filtered by the GATK variant filter module with the following strict filter settings. For indels, âQDâ&lt;â2.0 || FSâ&gt;â200.0 || ReadPosRankSumâ&lt;ââ20.0â. For SNPs âQDâ&lt;â2.0 || FSâ&gt;â60.0 || MQâ&lt;â40.0 || MQRankSumâ&lt;ââ12.5 || ReadPosRankSumâ&lt;ââ8.0â, with additional filtering steps including removing: (1) SNPs with more than two alleles; (2) SNPs at or within 5âbp from any indels; (3) genotypes with quality scores (GQ)â&lt;â10, or extremely low (&lt;one-third average depth) or extremely high (&gt;threefold average depth) coverage; (4) SNPs with more than two missing genotypes in either <i>O. rehderiana</i> or <i>O. chinensis</i>; and (5) SNPs showing significant deviation from HardyâWeinberg equilibrium (<i>P</i>â&lt;â0.001) in either of the two species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome-wide genetic diversity analysis</h3><p>Genome-wide heterozygosity, within-individual SNV incidence, and SNV density within 50-kb windows were calculated in the intergenic (putative neutral, 10âkb away from coding regions) and coding regions for all 32 individuals. Of 50-kb windows, 6768 and 6456 were used with a total length of 338.4âMb (92.4%) and 332.8âMb (86.9%) in the <i>O. rehderiana</i> and <i>O. chinensis</i> genomes, separately. Genetic diversity (<i>Ï</i>) was calculated using 50âkb sliding windows in 10âkb steps for datasets 1 and 2 by VCFtools v0.1.12b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156â2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2525">74</a></sup>. The other detailed information for calculating FROH, LD, the population recombination rate (<i>Ï</i>), and population demography are shown in theÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM1">supplement methods</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Estimates of neutrality and deleterious-derived alleles</h3><p>The genome-wide ratio of heterozygosity (heterozygotes/total genotypes) of zero to four-fold degenerate sites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Petit, R. J., Hu, F. S. &amp; Dick, C. W. Forests of the past: a window to future changes. Science 320, 1450â1453 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2544">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Marsden, C. D. et al. Bottlenecks and selective sweeps during domestication have increased deleterious genetic variation in dogs. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 113, 152â157 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2547">47</a></sup> was calculated within coding regions based on <i>O. rehderiana</i> annotation (dataset 3). The zero and four-fold degenerate sites were identified by iterating across all four possible bases at each site along a transcript and recording the changes in the resulting amino acid. Sites were classified as zero-fold degenerate when the four different bases resulted in four different amino acids, and four-fold degenerate when no changes in amino acids were observed. Then the ratio of zero-fold to four-fold degenerate site were calculated by each individual.</p><p>Prior to detection of deleterious variants, all segregating sites in dataset 3 were phased and imputed using BEAGLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Browning, S. R. &amp; Browning, B. L. Rapid and accurate haplotype phasing and missing-data inference for whole-genome association studies by use of localized haplotype clustering. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 81, 1084â1097 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2557">75</a></sup>, and SnpEff<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Cingolani, P. et al. A program for annotating and predicting the effects of single nucleotide polymorphisms, SnpEff: SNPs in the genome of Drosophila melanogaster strain w1118; iso-2; iso-3. Fly 6, 80â92 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2561">76</a></sup> was used to classify the SNPs based on the <i>O. rehderiana</i> annotation. To avoid reference bias when identifying derived alleles and deleterious variants, we only called the polarity of variants when all three outgroups (<i>C. cordata, C. fangiana</i>, and <i>O. nobilis</i>) had identical homozygous states. Nonsynonymous SNPs were assessed using PolyPhen2 (v2.2.2r405, database: UniRef100)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Adzhubei, I. A. et al. A method and server for predicting damaging missense mutations. Nat. Methods 7, 248â249 (2010)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2575">48</a></sup>, PROVEAN (v1.1.5, database: NR)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Choi, Y., Sims, G. E., Murphy, S., Miller, J. R. &amp; Chan, A. P. Predicting the functional effect of amino acid substitutions and indels. PLoS One 7, e46688 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2579">49</a></sup>, and SIFT (v6.0.1, database: UniRef90)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Ng, P. C. &amp; Henikoff, S. SIFT: predicting amino acid changes that affect protein function. Nucleic Acids Res. 31, 3812â3814 (2003)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2583">50</a></sup> with their default settings. The intersection of those three approaches may provide more accurate predictions than any single prediction approach alone, because each approach varies slightly in its prediction procedure and assumptions<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Hutchings, J. A. Thresholds for impaired species recovery. Proc. Biol. Sci. 282, 20150654 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d39496e2587">51</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Code availability</h3><p>The custom scripts have deposited in GitHub (<a href="https://github.com/yongzhiyang2012/Two_iron_wood_genome_analysis">https://github.com/yongzhiyang2012/Two_iron_wood_genome_analysis</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Reporting summary</h3><p>Further information on experimental design is available in theÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-018-07913-4#MOESM6">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The WGS projects have been deposited at NCBI GenBank under BioProject ID PRJNA428013 for <i>O. rehderiana</i> and BioProject ID PRJNA428014 for <i>O. chinensis</i>. The genomic sequencing data and transcriptomic raw data have been deposited in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under BioProject ID PRJNA428015 and PRJNA428018, respectively.</p>

Y.Y., Z.L., and M.Z. collected the materials. Z.L. and Y.L. prepared DNA and RNA for sequencing. T.M. and Y.Y. performed the genome assembly and genome annotation. J.L., T.M., and C.F. designed comparative genomics analyses. Y.Y, Z.W., and Y.L. performed comparative genomics analysis. Y.Y., Z.W., and Y.L. identified deleterious mutations. Y.Y, Z.L., M.Z.Â and X.C. built scaffolds and observed cymules. J.L., Y.Y., T.M., and M.S.O. wrote the manuscript with the input of all co-authors.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding author</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
